Total points: 1055 (Level 3)

<question>



What is a 2.1 in British education?



I recently started a course in Accounting and Finance Management at a British university and I was browsing through a booklet that explained jobs in the finance sector and they constantly keep referring to something called "2.1". What is it and what on earth does it stand for?



</question>



<answer from>



JB
Member since: 11 August 2008
Total points: 348 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



A 1st is over 70%
A 2:1 is 60 - 70%
A 2:2 is 50 - 60%
A 3rd is 40 - 50%
A fail is less than 40%



Most people get a 2:1, and that is the minimum grade usually required by graduate training schemes etc



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks, thats the info I was looking for.



</asker's comment>

<question from>



Bon B
Member since: 25 August 2008
Total points: 111 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Question bout college?



if my gpa is two points below the normal gpa for a student getting into the same college, but my SAT scores are above the avg for the college will my gpa effect me greatly? Is gpa the number one thing colleges look at?



</question>



<answer from>



kirbyeli...
Member since: 19 August 2008
Total points: 107 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Now days, most colleges use some type of formula. If you meet their minimum score based on the formula, you have a good shot. They usually multiply the GPA by some number and add the SAT score times another number. If you meet the minimum number those two have to equal up to, they will consider you. Usually, unless you are aiming for ivy league, that minimum number is not very high. Then, they look at extra-curriculars, essays, volunteer work, etc... I think volunteer work has become a big booster and honestly almost mandatory for most colleges lately. Try to pull your GPA up if you still have time. If not, write a killer essay. The SAT will help, also, because it shows potential. You may want to address the slip in GPA in your essay. Or attach a separate letter explaining (and have a good reason) why you had some grades slip. Then explain how you brought them up or are more determined as shown by....fill in the blank. I don't want to say just come up with some bull****, but that is a large part of it. Put the best sping on the circumstances.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



nice



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Core
Member since: 21 March 2008
Total points: 11172 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



They don't just care about GPA, they also look at standard test scores and they do take your essay into account; so a student with okay grades and an amazing personality does have the possibility of getting in over someone with amazing grades, but a drab essay.



</answer>



<answer from>



drip
Member since: 16 March 2008
Total points: 9947 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Colleges don't look a one thing or one score anymore. They want the whole picture of the student. Your GPA, SAT, what classes you took in high school and at what level. Did you take any AP classes. Hold any leadership positions in school or sports or clubs.



</answer>

<answer>



After reading everyone else's answers, it occurred to me that it took me about the first term to have really found my feet.  By then I was settled in the course, had made friends (although I cheated as a friend from home had gone to the same uni a year before), joined societies and could finally understand the local accent - I went to uni in the north east of England!



The best bit is a year later when you know exactly how to hit Freshers' Fair to get the best free stuff, when you can help out the first years with translating in shops and finding their own feet, and then you know you're truly settled there!



Give it at least two weeks before you make a trip home if you can as it'll give you time to make the break between home and uni.  Most of all, enjoy yourself and remember that you're not alone.



Source(s):



Was a new uni student once upon a time.



</answer>

<answer from>



CattyOne
Member since: 04 October 2006
Total points: 1211 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



I think it depends what you expect. Also depends on your course - i.e. how many people are there. Generally if you live in halls you make friends quickly, unless you don't have the same interests and some people are just very different from what you will be used to, some are party animals, some religious, some not, some smokers and so on.



You will find people you get on better with eventually, and they may not be the same people you hang around with by the end of the year when you know everyone. So chill and enjoy things for now, freshers week etc
Join societies, clubs, voluntary stuff etc - Just make sure it's stuff you like, normally you meet people there - especially during 1st - 2nd week because they are most likely there to network too.



Enjoy



</answer>

<question>



Is a phD or masters higher?



which degree is more education and allows you to do more?



</question>



<answer from>



Scooter
Member since: 19 December 2007
Total points: 1162 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Depends. PhD is a "terminal" degree and generally higher than a masters. It matters what career you pursue. PhDs specialize and may limit you to the field of study in the doctorate. Masters degrees may be a bit more flexible.



Source(s):



I have a masters - friend has a PhD



</answer>



<asker's comment>



great thanks



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



OJ
Member since: 13 May 2007
Total points: 315 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



You must earn a Master's degree to earn a Ph.D. Therefore, a Ph.D is the highest level of education you can reach. With a Ph.D, you can teach in schools, universities, and hold higher-ranking positions (you are usually given more priority because of your age and educational superiority). Ph.D holders usually have more experience and have completed more research associated with earning this degree.



Source(s):



My father has a Ph.D



</answer>

<question from>



heizelsk...
Member since: 29 September 2008
Total points: 97 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Which course is harder? nursing or physical therapy?



what course should i choose?='c



</question>



<answer from>



Carly E
Member since: 08 September 2008
Total points: 296 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I'm not sure, i know that physical therapists need to know all the same as nurses, physiology and anatomy etc. the only difference is the clinical experience.



Source(s):



I'm a nurse and my friend's mother is a PT



</answer>



<answer from>



mistify
Member since: 12 August 2006
Total points: 33882 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



It's like comparing apples and oranges. Each requires a different skill set. Nursing requires more declaritive and procedural memory...side effects and interactions of medication, how to administer certain procedures/medications, etc. Nurses have to be able to multi-task and handle several situations at once.



Physical therapy requires a lot more analysis, cirtical thinking, problem solving and investigation.



So, it really depends on one's skills as to which one is "harder."



As far as compeititveness, physical therapy is traditionally a more competitive and stringent application process and courseload.



Source(s):



I am a PT



</answer>

<question from>



missd
Member since: 29 September 2008
Total points: 97 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Is it better to work in a Hospital or volunteer in a Hospital to get into Medical School?



I volunteer in a local hospital but i tried to get a job in a hospital. But unfortunately no hospital is hiring me. They say that there is no positions open at the hospital. So now i work in a day care school as an assistant teacher. I am a sophomore in college doing pre-med. Do you think that only volunteer work and no job in a hospital will help me in my medical application? Would working in the day care school do me any benefit towards my medical application?



</question>



<answer from>



Yggdrasi...
Member since: 15 September 2008
Total points: 1239 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



It is better to get excellent grades in your science prereqs. and general courses. Winning an academic award or two, or being in an academic honorary (like Phi Beta Kappa) would also be nice. You must also get great MCAT scores and write a killer essay (if required). All of your references should be original and say that you basically walk on water and will be the best MD since Jonas Salk. Hard to believe, but being a varsity athlete also helps. Outside work counts only to an extremely minor degree. And I cannot overemphasis getting the best liberal arts education you can get.



Edit: Someone mentioned going to nurse training first. BAD idea...it will be a BIG negative in your application. "Well, s/he trained as a nurse...now s/he wants to leave that to become an MD? When will he decide s/he wants to be a lawyer?" I know that sounds flip, but I know how admissions committees work.



And, about the major...it doesn't need to be a science major. For example, my dentist's major was Agricultural Economics.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



nice



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



science chick
Member since: 11 February 2008
Total points: 7485 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Health Care



</answer from>



<answer>



It depends on what you were doing. Most patient care jobs require some training. If you managed to get trained as an aide, either through on the job training in the hospital or through a CNA course, or if you got a job doing some other sort of patient care, it would look better than volunteering to just sit around with people or deal with assembling stuff. If you are actually doing something as a volunteer and you would only get getting a job in the kitchen, then its better to keep volunteering.  Reception work may not have patient car but may have you doing more than volunteering. Do whatever exposes you to more patient care, or at least to more of the hospital workings.



ADD: Yggdrasil brings up an excellent point. Only take a job if you can still get As in your science courses. If it distracts from your science courses, stick to volunteering. You should have something since most people will have at least volunteered, but science courses are the #1 priority and if you are struggling you need to have a flexible schedule.



</answer>



<answer from>



Shadow
Member since: 31 May 2007
Total points: 165 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



You should see if you can become a research assistant for any professors for your school. It is a pretty common thing for undergraduates to do. See if they have a free EMT program (like they train you and you work certain amount of events for them). I would also listen to the other advice as well.



</answer>

Aimee R

</answer from>



<answer>



It depends on your overall course selections.  If you have a number of courses that require a lot of outside reading, lots of papers or include labs, you will have to be very careful to manage your time so you can devote enough to each course.  If one or more of your courses are in subjects that you might struggle to understand, you definitely need to make sure you have enough time - and energy - to study more than usual.



If you are at the point where you have some flexibility in course selections, add one that really appeals to you, or would be fun to take or you've had the teacher in a previous class so you already know what his/her expectations are...then go for it.



18 credits is not too difficult to handle if you make good decisions about course selections. I did it for a number of consecutive semesters and still maintained a high GPA.



Good luck in your studies!



Source(s):



25 years higher ed admission and advising administration, faculty



</answer>

HOW IS THE FUTURE OF THE BIOTECHNOLOGY?



what are the offers we have?

</answer from>



<answer>



Scope and Importance of Biotechnology - Biotechnology has its newest roots in the science of molecular biology and microbiology. Advances in these two areas have been exploited in a variety of ways both for production of industrially important biochemicals (including enzymes) and for basic studies in molecular biology.



Therefore, a new commercial environment has been created, in which many famous scientists including some Nobel Laureates (like Walter Gilbert) opted to work for biotechnology companies. Scores of these companies are created and closed down every year.



In USA, alone there are more than 200 such companies including Genentech, Cetus, Hybritech, Biogen, etc. USA., Japan and Europe are leaders in biotechnology research and development.
Kindly hear some public sessions on wiziq.com.

My own experience and net.

<original file>7049.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk16724.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



LuXy In Love!
Member since: 27 November 2006
Total points: 1971 (Level 3)



</question from>



<question>



University of Phoenix question..Another one...?



One of the things the U of P hypes is that they claim employers to be in high demand of people from their universities.  Now I am not so naive as to believe every word they say, but am curious instead to see how students view this statement.  They proclaim that having you work in learning teams is one of the most desirable traits employers look for.  Also, that because you write so many papers there, that employers are looking for developed writing skills as well.  Is this true?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



They want to know if you can pay your tuition and that's the extent of their commitment to you. UOP spends over 400 MILLION dollars a year on advertising and marketing. It is one thing they do very, very well.



Yes, learning teams are a great way to develop desirable team/collaborative traits that employers like...but all colleges require team projects.  Yes, writing skills are valued...but all colleges require writing skills. This is nothing unique to UOP...it's marketing talk.



One problem with UOP's learning team concept is they do not have effective ways in which to monitor and evaluate individual contributions against the entire team's performance.  If you get stuck with a person who doesn't pull their weight, the entire team is penalized.  They call it an opportunity to learn how to work and manage teams.  I call it complete disregard of their responsibilities to ensure the quality of instruction and education an individual is expecting, and paying for, is  received.



I am strongly biased against UOP and their unethical practices relating to how they recruit students with false promises, shady statistics and poor student retention efforts.



Employers do not flock to UOP graduates; many companies have policies that specifically exclude graduates from UOP from applying. There are also companies who don't care where a degree is earned.



To be fair, I know people who earned degrees from them and had good experiences and others who either dropped out or realized after the fact that they earned a degree from a school that does not have a good reputation in the business world.



There is an interesting website of student/employee opinions of UOP.



Check it out: UOPsucks.com  The volume of posts and the fact that many of the same topics are continually commented on makes for interesting reading.



Keep investigating so you can make informed decisions.  Regardless of where you enroll, good luck in your studies.



Source(s):



25 years higher ed administration-admissions and advising, faculty



</answer>

<question from>



desiqt78...
Member since: 05 September 2008
Total points: 268 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Which Florida college has a better premed program?



USF, UCF, or FSU?  I would try for UF, but I'm not nearly qualified enough to get accepted.



Also what are my chances at getting accepted to any of the above?



Unweighted GPA: 3.038
Weighted GPA: 3.356
(very low grades freshman year, but high grades sophomore and junior year)
SAT MATH: 670
SAT READING: 650
SAT WRITING: 560



</question>



<answer from>



roaringm...
Member since: 12 December 2005
Total points: 41542 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Apply to U Florida, if you like it. Don't rely on your overall GPA. Yes, apply to schools that fit your overall GPA, but also apply to one or two schools that seem to be a reach for your overall GPA, but that fit your GPA from your sophomore and junior years.



The fact that you did well in your sophomore and junior years will be weighted quite heavily by university admissions. In addition, your strong SAT scores will support that. Don't *NOT* apply to U Florida because your overall GPA is too low, if your sophomore and junior GPA is in their range!



Have I hammered that in enough? :LOL: Apply to U F. I think you might actually have a shot there. And U F has the strongest pre-med program of those you listed.



When you're looking at schools, ask them what percentage of their applicants to US med schools actually get in. That'll tell you which one has the strongest pre-med program. But in general, if med school is your goal, go to the strongest uni you can get into and that fits you well, as things like your performance at uni, and the academic reputation of your undergrad program, matter in terms of med school admissions.



Thus I'd probably rank your choices as follows, but again, ask the unis what their percent admit rate is re: med schools:
- U Florida
- U Miami
- Florida State U
- New College of Florida
- Rollins College
- and then UCF and USF



You'll noticed I added in a couple for your consideration.



Be very specific in the question you ask them. Ask, “Of every 100 students who start out pre-med, what percent end up actually attending medical school?”



Make sure they don't answer "Oh, 90% of *qualified* applicants..." No. Wrong answer.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks, that really helped a lot :)



</asker's comment>

<answer from>



opiniono...
Member since: 31 August 2008
Total points: 996 (Level 2)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



I suggest you build on your undergraduate degree and look for a master's program that, at the least, has a program title that is reflective of content and that offers focused studies in a discipline that gives you greater marketability. If you are interested in Strategic Leadership, get a business degree or MSHRM; teaching or training...a Masters in Education.



You run a very real risk of a potential employer looking at the words "Master of Professional Studies" and wondering what the heck that means and dismissing your acheivements. It sounds like a graduate degree in being a professional student! You should not have to start a job search by having to explain your degree. Content confusion can be the difference between getting the interview or having your resume being tossed. Plus, in the age of electronic resume submissions, your resume can be filtered out because the degree name doesn't match any category the computer is programmed to read as valid.



Colleges do a disservice to students by labeling degree programs with ambiguous names. In a blatent effort to generate graduate programs that have mass appeal (ie; lots of revenue) and marketing them as opportunities for students to "focus their studies to match their career goals", degrees like this often end up with watered down content and little credibility. The appeal of the degree often lies in the flexible scheduling and ease of learning and not in its content, which is exactly what the school is hoping you buy into.  Graduate programs are inherently designed to focus, not generalize, advanced studies.  If the degree is offered by a for-profit online institution (like U of Phoenix, for example), you are further crippling your marketability.  There is nothing wrong with on line learning; it is an excellent option for working adults....just be very cautious as to the school's reputation, the quality of instruction and ultimately, the return on investment.



Ask the school for a list of students who have graduated from this program. Find out how many obtained jobs as a result of this specific degree...not just getting a job because a Masters was required, or a list of who were promoted as a result of earning this specific degree.  Make them show you hard data that supports the marketability of an MPS degree.  If they can't, well...



As to your question, which really is asking about continuing your studies...yes, by all means go for a master's degree...just not one in Professional Studies.



Please excuse my rant, but after years of working in higher ed, this trend of offering subpar degrees that serve to generate revenue at the expense of students just enrages me.



Source(s):



25 years higher ed admissions and advising administration, faculty



</answer>

<question>



3.43 GPA, 1920 on the SAT's, 29 on the ACT's, etc... Which colleges would accept me?



I know not unless you are a college admission administrator most people have to "guess" what their chances are for certain colleges. Many people says a 3.43 GPA is average.



To give you some background info, most of my classes were Honors and AP classes, and just a few reg. courses here and there throughout my 3 years of high school so far. I took one SAT so far and got a 1920, I'm going to take it again in October and maybe for the third time in November. I ended up taking the ACT's and got a 29. If it helps I live in NJ, Volunteered 100+ hours, joined several clubs and were dedicated to them. I won a couple of Best Poetry awards during high school, and one of my poems was published in a poetry book called Tracing the infinite. I am sure that I got the best recommendation my teachers and counselor could give me, and I will work hard to write a great essay.



Now that I'm going to be a senior in HS this year with many other people, I'm looking for which colleges to apply to, and trying to determine which is a reach safety and match. Sometimes I feel so down whenever I hear people with 3.7+ GPA, which they earned no doubt about it. Still after I hear those GPA's it makes me wonder what kind of college would accept me.

I was wondering if these colleges would be a reach, safety, or match for me. Before some of you answer I do not want to go to community college and transfer.

Rutgers
Villanova
Boston University
Lehigh
Montclair
TCNJ

I know most of them are great schools and tough to get into. Out of all of them Villanova is my first choice.



</question>



<additional details>



Oh and to some people who think I'm trying to make them do all the work. Did you read? I'm looking for which colleges would be good hence I am doing research on it. I just wanted a second opinion aside from websites, teachers and etc. When I look at the answers I could tell what was a well thought out answer or just BS.



</additional details>



<answer from>



tpoplar
Member since: 08 June 2006
Total points: 843 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I'm a graduate of Boston University, and I'm pretty certain you'll be accepted there. I would try to get that GPA up to at least a 3.5 or 3.6 (I had a 3.8 unweighted) if you want to have any kind of academic scholarship. I think sometimes students are so concerned with just getting accepted into a certain university, they forget to consider how they'll finance going to those schools if they do get accepted. Boston University's tuition skyrockets ever year (I think cost of attendace is around $47,000 a year). If you don't get an academic scholarship--and if you aren't rich--then just how do you plan on financing that education?
This is basically the only reason I tell you to get your GPA up. BU will consider you for their scholarships if you have at least a 3.5 or 3.6, but if you're just concerned about getting in, then I don't think you'll have a problem with that. 
Your hard factors (i.e. test scores, GPA, class rank) look pretty good, and I'd say your soft factors (i.e. volunteer work, extracurriculars, essay) are looking great as well. While I don't think you'll get into any Ivy League schools with those stats (although you never know), I'd definitely say you're on par with the schools you're looking to apply to.

Break a leg!



Source(s):



Graduate of Boston University (class of 2008); was given a full scholarship including the Boston University grant, Claflin scholarship, music award, and was the recipient of the Horatio Alger National scholarship (look into it, it's not a BU award, but they give you $20,000 based on income and an easy essay). Good luck, and feel free to contact me with any other questions.



</answer>

<question>



What are the millenium development goals in science.



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



There are 8 millennium development goals:
1.  Eradicate extreme poverty and hunger
2.  Achieve universal primary education
3.  Promote gender equality and empower women
4.  Reduce child mortality
5.  Improve maternal health
6.  Combat HIV/AIDS, malaria, and other diseases
7.  Ensure environmental sustainability
8.  Develop a global partnership for development

I think that goals 4-8 all have a huge basis in science.  You can read more about each of these at the link below.



Source(s):



http://www.un.org/millenniumgoals/



</answer>

<answer from>



astrobuf
Member since: 01 July 2007
Total points: 4853 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Yes, to some extent.



If you graduate with a useful degree (business, engineering, medicine, law (maybe) from a major/respected school, starting salaries can be considerably higher. A Harvard Law or MBA Graduate can start 2X or even more than a graduate of a mid tier school. Graduates of prestigeous schools may advance faster (in part due to competence) due to connections and networks made while at these schools.

For mid tier schools (i.e most major Public Universities, significant private universities) salaries will not vary greatly.

For lower tier universities there can be a large difference in salaries or for that matter a graduates ability to get a good job at all. Many companies do not bother to recruit at smaller schools or those with below average reputations. A graduate of one of these schools may still advance on their own competence, but the road is steeper and there are more obstacles.

Astrobuf



Source(s):



30 years as an Engineering R&D  Manager and recruiter.



</answer>

<answer>



It isn't true that an employer will pay more to an employee who graduated from one school rather than another.  However, graduates of top schools often have more options available to them, so they can pick the best offer and often make more than others to begin with because of that.

$44,000 is pretty good - it sounds well above the average to me, but it may depend on what majors are offered there.  Why would you not stay to graduate?  If you are concerned about salary, you can almost guarantee a higher one with a college degree than without one.



</answer>

<answer from>



Angel
Member since: 14 August 2007
Total points: 15424 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



Dissertations are fun to write.

1. You need to do is choose a subject you are interested in or want to research.

2. Planning is key to the success of the dissertation or any other body of writing. Learn to use your word processor very well and research the referencing system you need to use.

3. Working consistently and methodically every day is the only way to get the work done on time and to a high standard. Reading and re-reading your work correcting, honing and re-working is all part of the work you need to do.
Link :  http://books.google.co.uk/books?id=d-9A9...

Link :  http://uk.youtube.com/watch?v=CdhzwS2cuP...

Link : http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0huiPI5R-...



</answer>



<answer from>



philipsc...
Member since: 12 May 2007
Total points: 3958 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



As someone has already said - start with something you are interested in.  It's a big help six months down the line.]

Most dissertations suffer from a lack of focus - they are to broad.  The best ones have a very narrow focus that allows detailed quality work.  The into and conclusions allow you to put this narrow study into a wider context (showing real world value).

You don't have to completely solve a problem (in most cases) but develop some knowledge.

Good luck



Source(s):



20 years of marking dissertations and projects



</answer>

<answer>



Contrary to belief, GCSE's are actually still important when applying to university. You may have excellent A-Level results but it is preferable to choose a candidate with both excellent A-Levels and GCSE's. A grade of C or above is required in Mathematics and English and often in Science's too. English language though is particularly important for studying Law as I am sure you are fully aware. Good luck with your application and I hope you are successful. 
ps Obviously standards differ between universities but if you intend to apply to more prestigious universities such as Oxbridge, Nottingham, Durham, York, Sheffield and so on then your grades are of paramount importance. x



Source(s):



Currently studying for my second degree



</answer>

<question>



How do stay really focused in college? ?



I feel like im losing focused and it has only been my 3rd day going to an university. When i go to class i am absolutely clueless and this is realy bad espcially when im an econ major. I haven't been reading the book like I'm supposed to, but maybe i should. So what is the sort of mentality a thriving student would have when they are pursuing an education in economics?



</question>



<answer from>



YoU gOt OwNeD!! :)
Member since: 27 June 2008
Total points: 626 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



first of all, i wouldnt study economics...The economy is low already, just imagine how bad its gunna be about time you graduate and try to find a job.....

<answer from>



VP Debate = Comedy
Member since: 18 August 2008
Total points: 2198 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



Think of it this way:



The economy right now is horrible, right?



Well, if you graduate within the next four years, you will most definitely be welcomed in the job market with your economics degree. Economists will need to be innovative in their thought and would welcome some younger, fresh ideas.
Think of your possible success (and wealth - that is a motivation for many people, as it should be.)



Are you interested in your major? Most people tend to lose focus on the things they don't really like or appreciate. Is there something else you would rather be doing? It's never too late to change a major if you're willing to invest further time into it.



What I do to stay focused is make sure I get a dosage of every aspect of life so I don't feel like I'm missing out on anything. My number one tip for you to be is SCHEDULING. Purchase a planner, make it your life-line. Write down times you need to study, deadlines for school assignments, exam notices, write down time frames when you can have some time-off by relaxing or hanging out with friends.



You need to have some time to do other things that are non-academic in order to stay focused on the assignments that professors ask of you to do.



Mentality wise -- like I said, think of what a degree in economics would do for you, especially in today's society and economy.



Last but certainly not least: Research the career you plan on entering after graduation. What jobs are available to students with an economics degree? Ask questions like that, talk to advisers, professors, economists, etc. Get a feel for what you're getting in, and you might find something that will rejuvenate your motivation and make you more focused on what you are working to achieve.



It's your money that you're paying for school; most importantly, it is your TIME invested into this degree -- use those resources well, use them to the fullest and don't let the next years drag by. Think positive. :)



Those are all things I did, I hope I helped.



Source(s):



I'm a premed student at the University of Michigan, working on a bachelor of arts in psych and bachelor of science in bio and a minor in art history -- trust me, I need all the motivation I can get. :) Those are all tips I use.



</answer>

<question>



What literature should i read to stand me in good stead to get into UCL to do an English Degree? ?



i'm doing my alevels. 
UCL = University College London

its highly regarded and i really want to be accepted.



</question>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



Read as widely as you can. By all means focus on what interests you, but make sure you've also read a good balance of historic and contemporary literature. Chaucer, Shakespeare, Swift, and Austen are good bets. Think about what qualities make them enduring, and what modern writers might share, or aspire to, or rebel against these qualities, as revealed by their work. Develop a few ideas about what literature tells us about other ages, and about our own. Show that you can think and talk intelligently about your reading and you'll do well. Best of luck!



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



The way I see it, just read a good range of literature. No real specifics would be needed, but having a good broad knowledge and the ability to compare all sorts of styles and genres would be best.



</answer>



<answer from>



no answerer details



</answer from>



<answer>



whatever you're interested in.... think about choosing a certain topic and reading different books that go into it, for example I wrote about texts exploring dystopian society in my personal statement. UCL interviews so it's not so important to have read specific texts, but more to have an opinion on it. don't waste time trying to get through the really complicated stuff (ie. Joyce) if you don't enjoy it because you're bound to be able to talk more animatedly and in depth on a text you "get". oh, and read a few critical analysis texts, it's always good to see other people's opinions. I'd recommend some of Shelley's poetry personally... try "Ozymandias" if you don't know it already. It's short but throws up so many ideas. Good luck!



Source(s):



going to durham to read eng lit in october.

<answer>



Start with the Norton Anthology of English Literature. That should do it. It covers all the major writers from Chaucer to the present. It is huge though (in two volumes) but is essential for anyone wanting to study lit at degree level. Choose writers you enjoy and talk about those at interview. It wouldn't hurt to brush up on Shakespeare though.

<answer from>



philipsc...
Member since: 12 May 2007
Total points: 3958 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



You need to be clear on why you want to go.  If you are not clearly motivated then you are more likely to drop out (having taken on a loan that needs to be paid back).  What interests you?  What do you want to spend the rest of your life doing, what would you like to be called/labelled?  UCAS (look on the web) have guides that may help you to find a course you want.  If your interests have links to professional groups then they will also be able to help identify a course.  For example: buyers, accountants, doctors, actors, engineers, musicians, etc . all have organisations who you can contact for help.

Depending on the course you will need some evidence of ability.  This could be based on your work, which might show aptitude for a particular area, and ability with arithmetic (e.g. if you do calculations).  However, many courses require you to have maths and English GCSE passed at C grade or above.  This is necessary to cope with the level of work.  In addition, you might be asked to do either a foundation course, or another exam, e.g. HNC or A 'level.  This is to give you experience with academic work, without having to incur the expense of signing up to a course and finding it doesn't suit.  Don't think of this as a hurdle - it helps to get you into the learning habit, and lets you check that it's really what you want to do.

At 23, with some life behind you, you should be able to get more out of the experience.  If you've developed work discipline skills then these can be applied to uni: time keeping, application to tasks, self-management.

Talk to the universities you are interested in.  They will have an "Access" office.  This is intended to help people who are not from families with a uni history, people not coming straight from school, and others who need additional information and support.

Do it.  Some of my best students have been from non-typical starting points.  They've gone on to successful and fulfilling careers.



Source(s):



Had a number of jobs, but worked in unis for about 20 years.



</answer>



<answer from>

Member since: 04 August 2008
Total points: 113 (Level 1)

<answer from>



sashs.ge...
Member since: 16 November 2006
Total points: 3000 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Yes you can.

If you look at university web sites they will give A level requirements for entry but then they will have information for entry for mature students.

They look at your life experience and other skills.

BUT it depends on the course, you would struggle to do Uni level maths without doing A level first.

Going straight to uni is an enormous shock to the system. One of my uni lecturers started uni as a mature student with one O Level but says she wouldn't recommend it.

Access courses are designed for people like you, to bridge the gap between school leaving and uni. You could have a look at your local college for access courses.

Also some unis now do a 'foundation year', a sort of year 0 to get you up to uni year 1 standard.



You may also have more success at a 'new' uni. They have a lot of mature students and students who do not have traditional qualifications



</answer>

<question from>



Tom G
Member since: 12 July 2008
Total points: 72 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What Masters can i do with my degree?



I have a 3rd B A hons degree in media communications with permance art. I got this 5 years ago and it is so open ended i have never really used it, now I would like to specialise in something maybe psychology, but do i need to have a degree in the subject first? or need certains grades? any help on where i can find this info would be great!
Thanks



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



If you want a Masters with the degree you have, you would most likely want a Masters that is related to that degree.  Most people who get a masters in communication either become professors or researchers.  Since you have a media comm degree, perhaps you should look at some form of broadcasting programs (broadcast journalism, radio, news, etc).

As for psychology, to Master in psychology you would first need a BA or BS degree (depending on where you want to go in the field of psychology) before joining a Masters program since Masters level classes won't cover the basics you should have learned in undergrad.



</answer>

<question from>

Member since: 28 March 2008
Total points: 96 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



University Chancellors



What is the role of the Chancellor and a Pro-Chancellor at a University?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Chancellor is an honorary role with no 'real' power, usually a person who has made some real accomplishment albeit not as a academic. Examples are University of Huddersfield - Patrick 'Star Trek' Stewart and my Chancellor at Leeds Metropolitan University was Brendan Foster - a former long distant athlete. The main man is the Vice Chancellor, he is the CEO of the University. Usually a tenured Professor. e.g. at Leeds Met the Vice Chancellor is Prof. Simon Lee - Professor of Law.

Pro Chancellor are seconds to the Vice Chancellor and are usually drawn from the ranks of the most senior of academics of the University.

Hope this helps



Source(s):



LLB Law Graduate



</answer>

<question>



Help On Architecture???



Is It easy to change from a residential designer to a commercial one?
Is there anything on the computer or at home that I can do to practice for my future career in Architecture? (I'm 17 years old now)
What are other types of Architecture? (Bridge? etc...)
Do I have to be really good at freehand drawing or even art at all?
ETC.. Please I need a bunch of information even information that I have not asked you for thanks.



</question>



<answer from>



Brandon
Member since: 20 June 2008
Total points: 1844 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



You may want to rethink your career path. Here is why:

The Reality: Architecture has many pluses. Indeed, it made it (barely) into last year's list of Best Careers. Alas, for 2008, architecture must be moved to the overrated category. Not only is the housing decline souring the job market, but more potential clients are offshoring the work to India, downloading premade blueprints developed by top architects, or having lower-cost interior/exterior designers or building contractors design their structure. Two recent job satisfaction surveys painted a mixed picture: One gave architecture high marks, but the other rated it very low. A major cause of low job satisfaction is that many architects don't get to design buildings but rather must produce detailed drawings of the components of other people's designs, such as the heating, ventilation, and air-conditioning system.

Alternative: Interior Designer. Halfway between an architect and an interior decorator, interior designers recommend where a wall should go as well as the color scheme.



Source(s):



http://www.usnews.com/articles/business/...



</answer>

<question>



Will I be able to do Economics at University ?

Will I Be able to do Economics at University?

</answer from>



<answer>



All the good universities require a maths A level - it's possible that some of the former polytechnics do not, but if you want to go somewhere where you'll get a degree which means something, then you need to do the A level. If you can't take it at school, have you looked into doing it at a local college, and maybe dropping one of the others? Critical thinking is often not even accepted by universities, so dropping that and doing the maths A level at college would be an idea - colleges usually offer a one year course and have evening classes etc so it would be fairly easy to fit around your schoolwork. Good luck!

<answer>



Keeping things professional.

Your personal life and business life should never collide in the office. Keep things real - be aware that just about everyone is a game player and willing to stand on the next guy to get ahead.

Think before you open your mouth.

What is point of the SAT?

sharonsJ...
Member since: 19 July 2007

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I feel the same way about the SAT... always have, and always will. I too just finished my first year of college.



Apparently this test is supposed to be "fair." Testmakers try to make it as standard and general as possible, to not give any one student an advantage in the test material than the next. They don't want to be accused for being biased towards anyone, right.

But not all students can memorize and spit out the answer. Not all students think alike in what the more important "theme" is... after reading a passage.



There's a big difference between just memorizing something, and truly learning something.... and that is what sucks about the SAT, it doesn't acknowledge that. Everyone has to learn the same way, think the same way (their way) and it's kind of unfair, but I don't think we will see  any changes with that.



Source(s):



I've taken the SAT like 3 times!! I hated everything about it!

<answer from>



Jamie
Member since: 04 August 2008
Total points: 318 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Its not suppose to test your KNOWLEDGE, but rather your intuition and reasoning/problem solving. Though the problems you see on the test are not ones you will encounter in every day life or academia, they are a good assessment of intelligence rather than knowledge, to a certain extent.

In this way, colleges can have one standardized criteria for comparing people from completely different backgrounds or schools (because of grade inflation, etc, you need that standard).



If the test was on physics problems and chinese literature, then kids who study those specific topics would be at an advantage. Because it tests for general knowledge of math, writing and verbal, the only way to study is to simply gain awareness and general knowledge that you can acquire in any and all settings, although many kids try to short cut that approach by practicing the test itself and learning short cuts (not the intention of the exam).



</answer>



<answer from>



bab
Member since: 03 July 2008
Total points: 1124 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



It's pretty much an IQ test and it does correlate with success in college, though not as well as HS GPA does. The greatest correlation however is between SAT score and family income. Wealthy kids from highly educated families do best on the test.

The ACT is the test that measures what you've learned in classes. Kids who study hard but who are not as naturally smart do better on it.



</answer>

<answer>



It depends slightly on what examinations board you used for your GCSEs - they do vary somewhat. <evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Contrary to the poster above me, the Ivy League schools are not Fordham and Berkley. Ivy League grads do not think they are better than everyone else, either.



Here is the list of Ivy League members in order of prestige:
1. Harvard (MA)
2. Princeton (NJ)
3. Yale (CT)
4. University of Pennsylvania (PA)
5. Columbia (NY)
6. Dartmouth (NH)
7. Brown (RI)
8. Cornell (NY)

There are only 8 members of the Ivy League.  Opposite to what the person above says, they do not "costs megabucks," the financial aid is very generous and students rarely pay the sticker price.



All of the eight members are known for their academic excellence, high quality research, and diverse student body.



The Ivy League attracts qualified students from all over the world. One who applies needs more than an impeccable transcript and extra-curriculars.



One needs to demonstrate passion and character through essays, alumni interviews, and teacher recommendations. The Ivy League is also know to look beyond the numbers i.e. GPA, SAT scores, AP scores, SAT II scores, etc. They evaluate you as a person, too.



</answer>

<answer>



The Ivy League is an athletic conference comprising eight private institutions of higher education located in the Northeastern United States. The term is most commonly used to refer to those eight schools considered as a group. The term also has connotations of academic excellence, selectivity in admissions, and a reputation for social elitism.

<question>



How do you become a solicitor and what qualifications would i need?



Hi I'm in year 10 and would like to become a solicitor when i leave school, i was wondering what GCSE's i need, also what further education qualifications to,on the same hand what could i do to make sure i do succeed up to leaving school etc



</question>



<answer from>



princess...
Member since: 28 June 2007
Total points: 4530 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Books & Authors



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



It won't matter much what GCSEs you have, although good grades at Maths and English are always preferable.

You would need to have three good A-levels. Law is a very popular university course, and the better your A-level grades the better your chances of getting onto a course at a good university. It generally doesn't matter what subjects you do (although humanities based subjects are probably best). If your college or school does law A-level, it's not necessarily a good idea to do it. A lot of universities are wary about it as it has such a different format from degree level law.

The best course to take is generally the LLB law degree. You could check out the Law Society's website: http://www.lawsociety.org.uk/home.law



</answer>

talllegg...



States require agents to take a course prior to the licensing exam.  NO degree is required, but there are separate exams (life, health, property, casualty) for specific licenses.  Many agents today have licenses in other areas (i.e. securities) to provide investment opportunities for their clients.

<answer>



First of all, you should relax.  The first day of classes, professors for first year classes are expecting flustered students.  Even if you walk in late, do it quietly and sit at the back of the classroom.  It'll be fine.

Now as to the back-to-back classes situation, look at what building your classes are in.  Are they in the same one?  If so, the difference will probably be okay; explain to both teachers the situation, and hopefully they'll be willing to work with it.

However, if you go to a large university where classes can be ten minutes away by bus you might need to reschedule one of those classes.



Even if they're in buildings that are side by side, the time thing should be okay.  Relax, have fun, and focus on the college experience! Good luck!



</answer>

<answer from>



tpoplar
Member since: 08 June 2006
Total points: 836 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Let me just say that this is probably the most adorable question I've answered all day.
Colleges advisors are notorious for putting together horrible schedules (they basically don't care how you do it, but that you just get the credits in). Most professors understand this, and will let the class out 10 minutes ahead of time (so if your next class is scheduled from 1pm-3pm, the professor will let you out at 12:50pm in order to give you enough time to get to that class). Not all professors are this understanding however, and you might have to work something out with the professor personally, so that he/she knows you're leaving class early for a reason, not just because you're bored. 
Your freshmen year will be the worst by far, in part because freshmen are the last to choose their schedules. Once you become a sophomore, you'll have more freedom to choose a schedule that allows you enough time to get to each class, and might even offer you a break between classes (either to do homework, nap, or just relax before class).



I completely understand what you are going through. I'm from Alabama and went to Boston University in Massachusetts. I went by myself (read: no parental help), had to move in by myself, and had to figure out how to maneuver the campus on the subway by myself. It was a scary time, but after the first week or so, I had already begun to make friends and found that the freedom I was given was well worth the move and the hassle.

College is a big step financially and psychologically. It's a step many fail to take; so be proud that you've made it this far, and don't worry. Try to introduce yourself to the professor, as this will help him/her in not only remembering you, but also helps you to establish a rapport with them, which may come in handy if you need a recommendation later in your college career.

Remember: it's college, so no one is going to force you or check up on you to go to class. College will provide you with a lot of freedom, and it's up to you to decide whether you will use that freedom responsibly.

Break a leg!



Source(s):



Boston University double major (class of 2008).



</answer>

<answer>

They both have strong art programs and their ranking will carry your degree far. Also, I'm not sure about USC, but I know that UCLA's graphics design major is very good. It might even be better if you can do a minor or even double major in something computer science related, but that of course will be very hard. A bit farther, UC Irvine is also pretty good for graphics design.

merlina
Member since: 29 June 2007
Total points: 2045 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



When you say you've failed it three times, I presume you mean that you've used up all the permitted resit opportunities so have actually just failed it once (rather than having sat the year three times already)?

So, should you retake the year? Well there's a few things you need to look at:

Firstly, I'm concerned that you don't particularly enjoy your course. Why is this? Is the subject less interesting than you thought it would be? Is it just not taught very well at your Uni? If you can have a hard think about this it might help you come up with an answer.

Then, and this is probably the biggest factor, you need to think about why you failed - very honestly (after all, it's only you that you're answerable to). For eg did you not do the work because it wasn't interesting? Was it too hard? Did you just not work hard enough? Did you not understand what was required at level 2?



Obviously, there's no point in repeating the year if the same is going to happen again  - or if you scrape through and then the same happens in your third year.

Universities rarely 'expel' people! If you fail repeatedly, then it's more a case of you having not done what's needed to get to the next level. One of the way Unis are judged by the Government is on the number of people who progress (ie don't drop out for any reason) - hence they will give as many opportunities as is academically defensible to get through. It's not just about the money (money is always a factor in any commercial transaction!), a student who needs resits uses up lots of staff time so you're not actually that  profitable ;)

So, where do you go from here?
Well start thinking about alternatives. Do you have Plan B? ie what would you do if you don't get a degree? Would you be better to cut your losses, perhaps get a job for a year to pay off some of those debts, and then think about starting afresh at a new course you are more enthusiastic about in a year's time?  Do you need this degree for a career you've set your heart on? If so, would you better resitting your year at your present institition or starting over somewhere else?

I realise that I've posed more questions than given answers, but hopefully they will clarify your thinking

From where I'm sitting there doesn't seem to be a lot of point in redoing a year that you're not enjoying and aren't doing well at, but there might well be a whole host of factors I'm unaware of.

Finally, a decision making tip. Right now, tel yourself you've decided, say, to repeat your year. Live with that decision for a day or two. How does that feel? Like you did the right thing or do you have feelings of dread and depression. Now decide the opposite - you're going to leave. How does that feel after you've lived with that decision? Any better?



</answer>



<answer from>



daydream...
Member since: 06 June 2008
Total points: 4088 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



So basically, the class isn't the issue: it's your money.

I suggest you get your financial situation better before even attempting to take this class again for the fourth time (if you haven't used up all of your "lives").



</answer>

<answer>



it's not down to which one is most interesting or better paid, it's which one interests YOU the most.  what's the point in doing all that hard work to go into a career that you end up hating/severely disliking if all you get is good money. it's no way to live a life, and you are in an excellent position to make sure you don't end up that way.

for example.  i couldn't make up my mind whether to do a degree in environmental sciences or Psychology and criminology.  both interest me highly, and appeal to me in different ways.  but i envisaged which one i could see my see myself working in.  and chose environmental science, but plan to do a diploma in psychology & criminology in the years after my degree - i could never see myself working in that field, it's purely out of personal interest.

read up on the careers that all those degrees can lead to and see how you feel then.  plus don't forget that most degrees have an extra years allowance - if you do most of the first year but decide you don't like the course you can change and still do a full degree.

<answer from>



sanjeev kumar singh

<answer>



i want to give advice about english this is very important thing for today  and think this will be forever
Effective Communication Fundamentals
Communication is the complex two-way process, involving encoding, translation and the decoding of the messages. The effective communication requires a communicator to translate their messages in the way which is specifically designed for the intended audience.
Creating and delivering the effective presentation requires basic understanding of a communication process. Most of the business presentations require a clear and an unambiguous communication of the message in the way which can be clearly understood by a recipient.



Tps for Effective Communication



·



Be honest while communicating. Dishonesty will somewhere show up along a line.



·



Take interest in the people you are communicating with. Remember the people are more attracted towards those who have interest in them, and pays more attention to what they say.



·



Think before you speak or put pen to paper: what message you trying to convey? What outcome do you want to elicit?



·



Be direct and not aggressive. Lot of flannelling around can make the people lose interest and miss a vital point.



·



Don't use the jargon – and acronyms, and also the technical expressions, unless you are sure about that your listeners do understand



·



Write the way as you will speak. Do not fall into a trap of using the long words just because it is written down.



·



Take time. Whether in the speech or in paper, rushing will make you seem nervous, unconfident and like downright scared.



Ten Tips for Good Interpersonal Skills



Listen to the person first. Communication is the two-way process; getting all your message across depends on understanding a other person. 
Be interested in people you will be communicating with. Remember that the people are more attracted towards those who have interest in them, and therefore will pay more attention to what they will say. 
Relax. The bad body language like hunched shoulders, fidgeting, the toe-tapping or the hair-twiddling all give a game away. 
Smile and use the eye contact. It is a most positive signal which you can give. 
Ask the questions. It is great way to show the people that you really are interested in them. 
If the other person has different point of view towards you find out why they have such point of view. More you understand reasons behind their thinking more you will be able to understand their point of view or can help them understand your point of view. 
Be assertive. so that we can try to value their input as your own inputs. Do not be pushy and do not be a pushover. Try for a right balance. 
When you will be speaking try to be enthusiastic in appropriate context. Use voice and the body language to show this. 
Immediately don't try to latch to something which someone has just now said ... "oh yes it happened to me" and immediately go on and telling your own story. Make sure that you ask questions about them first and then be careful while telling your story so as not to sound like a competition. 
Learn from the interactions. If you have a good conversation with someone try to think why it all went well and remember key points for the next time. If it did not go well - again try and learn something out of it.
Body Language
We all do communicate with one another through our look as well as what we do say and how do we sound. In fact what our body is doing while we are talking (i.e. the body language) could often play much greater part in the communication than we do think.



Most obvious form of the paralanguage is the body language or the kinesics . This is a language of the gestures, expressions, and the postures. In North America, for the instance, we commonly use arms and shake hands and say good-bye, point, count, express an excitement, beckon, warn away, a threaten, etc. In fact, we will learn many subtle variations of each of the gestures discussed above and make use of these gestures situation. We will use head to either say yes or to say no, to smile, frown, and wink acknowledgement or flirtation. The head and the shoulder in combination may shrug to indicating that we do not know something about the topic.



The eye contact
The eye contact helps to create the better interaction and the rapport with the listeners. Always try to look at listener at the end of the sentence to reinforce a message in that sentence.



The gesture
The gestures can help to give your voice the extra energy and the confidence Try to gesture on some of the key words – this will give the words a greater emphasis.



The Presence
Adopt ‘Anchor Position’ whenever you do want to keep the body language calm and controlled. While sitting do keep the small of back into back of the chair. This will help and support your posture and do maintain the energy and the confident style. Aim to keep the body lan



Source(s):



books and individual
sanjeev kumar singh



</answer>

<question from>



calculus...
Member since: 01 March 2008
Total points: 430 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



What classes should one take in college to prepare for medical school?



</question>



<answer from>



roaringm...
Member since: 12 December 2005
Total points: 40717 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Does your university offer a pre-med advisor? Make it a priority to meet with that person so you can plan out the classes and the volunteer experiences (like physician shadowing, etc.) that med schools will require of you, as an applicant.



To get into med school, you must first complete a certain series of classes, which always includes:



* 1 year general chemistry with labs



* 1 year organic chemistry with labs



* 1 year introductory biology with labs



* 1 year general physics with labs



And usually includes:



* College level math, sometimes including calculus



* 1 year English

Note that, if you did AP exams and think you could use some of those credits to fill some of these requirements, that's not good enough for some med schools. Some med schools require that you have actually taken the class in college, or to have taken a more advanced version of that class. Again, speak to your pre-med advisor for clarification on that one.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



wow thanks!!!!!



</asker's comment>

<question>



Which uni in the U.K offers the best postgraduate program? Which one is more employable? ?



G’day everyone,

I finished my bachelor’s degree two years ago in Taiwan and now I am thinking of doing my master’s in the U.K. Since I’d like to start next fall, I’m starting to look at some unis and just realized there are heaps of schools out there and I’m having the hardest time making a decision. So, as you can see, I’m v. desperate for some advice. I’ m mainly interested in areas such as media, culture, and communication (I had a B.A in English Literature.) and if possible, I’d like to work there for a year or two afterwards. I’m hesitating between Manchester, Nottingham, Leeds, and York. I know lots of ppl just can’t stop raving about Manchester, but how is the job market over there, in the sector of media industry?  What about Nottingham, they have an interesting program in cultural studies but I’ve been told it’s kinda seedy over there. I’m not looking to go on studying afterwards, so which uni is more employable? Would it be difficult for a intl graduate student like me to get a proper job there? I’m multilingual ( Mandarin, English, and French) and I’m more than happy to do entry-level job (admin, for example) and work my way up. Or, better yet, are there any other unis out there that might be more suitable for me? (London is definitely out of the picture since it’s ridiculously pricy for me.) Any suggestions r welcome and will be greatly appreciated. Thanks in advance. :-)



</question>



<answer from>



konic
Member since: 06 September 2008
Total points: 152 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I know from personal experience that all 4 of those universities are excellent! Like all cities they will have their seedy areas, but I spent the last year in Nottingham at uni as a post-grad and loved it.  York probably has the "quietest" night life in the way of big city clubbing, but has a thriving postgraduate community.



Check out the website www.findamasters.com to be able to search for masters.  My advice is do something you can be really passionate about, that doesn't pigeon hole you too much in a small subject area.

It would be very difficult for you to get a job whilst doing an MA/MSc full time due to the changing schedules of your work and the large work load.  Your visa as a non UK/EU student would likely have a work restriction in the region of 15 hours a week anyway.

Hope you decide to come here to study and good luck with your choice!



</answer>

Just say to your lecturer that you're so sorry you've been off, and explain that you feel you're kinda lagging behind with the work, and enquire as to whether or not there is anything you could do at home to catch up, or alternatively, dedicate some time from your lunch break for a week?

<answer from>



smack
Member since: 07 July 2006
Total points: 1231 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Talk to your professor and express your strong desire to get caught up with the work you missed because of illness. Set up a schedule with the professor for getting the work finished that you can actually accomplish. If you missed lectures, ask another student for notes, or ask the professor for a meeting to discuss the key points that you missed. If you show that you really want to get caught up, and follow through with any plans that you set up to get the work done, the professor should be understanding and even impressed.

In the future, contact the professor or another student before missing class to arrange for someone to take notes for you and get assignments for you.



Source(s):



I teach college students



</answer>

<question>



Am I wasting my time studying for an HND?

<answer>



It depends on what sort of job you are planning to do. As far as I understand it, an HND is more for vocational jobs, while you can get a degree to suit almost anything. An HND is equivalent to a normal bachelors degree, while some jobs while require you to have an honours degree. Also, I think you may have to have a job in order to do the HND (my partner is doing an HNC, and he is released from work one day a week in order to attend college).

If you know what job you are looking to do, try searching some job sites and the jobs advertised should tell you what qualifications you need to apply.

Emily K

<answer>



I think that by "city" university, you mean something close like a community college. The advantages to these are : smaller class sizes, less costly, close to home. Disadvantages: don't get the feel of "campus life", can't live in a dorm and see your friends all the time (unless you commute). I think that it's great to start out at a community college if you're going straight from high school. This is a big transitioning period, and you won't get shocked from being on a big university campus. On the other hand, if you're sure you're ready and want the true college experience, go for the university. You can eat on campus, be in sports/activities, make close friends, and have a blast if you manage your time right and stay on top of your studies. For me,that didn't work out because I got too wrapped up in alcohol, staying out late, bars, etc. I wound up back in community college :) Know yourself, research the schools, and find YOUR best fit. Good luck!



</answer>

<answer from>



Thesmile...
Member since: 12 July 2006
Total points: 3603 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Advantages of a city are that you are in the middle of a city, especially good if you can walk home from night clubs fairly easily/safely, also good for shopping. However with a campus you have everything you need quite close together (and sometimes they also have better student facilities. It is possible to get the best of both worlds, for example in Newcastle both campuses (for the University of Newcastle and Northumbria University) are actually in the city centre. Some have campuses which aren't too far, my uni (University of Birmingham) is about 40 mins walk from the city centre, but that means we still have to get taxis home from nights out. Some are really out of the way, Warwick for example seems to be in the middle of nowhere! Most have good public transport links to the city centre though.



</answer>

<question from>



lostone
Member since: 06 October 2007
Total points: 1630 (Level 3)



</question from>



<question>



Alpha Phi Omega demerit question?



so i am pledging this coed fraternity and i was caught without my book today and the brother was going to give me a demerit but i didn't have my paper so i might get one later.  basically  do you think this will have a big impact on weather or not i get voted in as a full brother?



</question>



<answer from>



Bubbling Brown
Member since: 11 March 2006
Total points: 167 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



As an Alumni Brother and Life Member of many decades, I can share with you my experience, as an undergrad,  with a Pledge of our chapter. This particular pledge brought his book for me to sign and in the course of a short conversation, I asked a question about the National history. The pledge did not know the answer, and I declined to sign at that time. He re-approached me on several subsequent occasions and, on each, I asked the exact same question, and on each, he still did not know. I never did sign his book, but when I asked if he felt that I was unfair in my expectations and treatment...he said NO, I was not.
At the Brotherhood meeting for voting on pledges, I stated my experience with this pledge and added that while I would not vote FOR him, I also would not vote AGAINST.
Keep in mind that Alpha Phi Omega is not a "social" fraternity, does not practice hazing, and generally does not practice "blackball" voting.
It appears to me that you are sincerely concerned about your oversight and I would expect that, if you apply yourself to the requirements of your pledge period, and demonstrate an interest in becoming a Brother of Alpha Phi Omega, you will find that a demerit or two along the way should not have much impact at all.



</answer>

thanks, you made me feel much better



</asker's comment>

<current file name>yqa_uk50277.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



Poppymir...
Member since: 01 October 2008
Total points: 107 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Shall i drop out of college? (aged seventeen)?



I've been at college for about a month now, And at first it was a novelty...
I saw my friends and met new people, and the work was ok.
Now i've just lost all motivation, I was off very sick for a week, and when i went back i couldn't get back into the work, i don't like getting up in the morning to go to college..  It's really depressing how stressful or samesame the work is, I'm taking Travel and Tourism, and Leisure and Tourism, The course promised a few trips but the nearest one is in MARCH, and thats to a theme park. I was hoping to see the world with my eyes, not through a screen. Its all computer work see - I'm also taking ICT and Maths Key Skills, But don't get me started on those...



Shall i swap courses, drop out, or keep going?



I'd appreciate any advice



</question>



<answer from>



teresath...
Member since: 04 April 2006
Total points: 51583 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



It's possible your bordeom and lack of motivation is just an after-effect of your illness.  Your body is still struggling, and doesn't have the energy that would normally make these tasks seem easy.  Give yourself time to recuperate before making any long-term decisions.



If you enrolled in college just for the field trips, then education is wasted on you, and your career will be a waste too.  The challenge of learning something new should excite you, not bore you to tears.  If you really don't like the subject, then try a different one.  That's the beauty of college - you have an infinite number of fields to explore.  So go find one that excites you.



Still, no major and no career is going to allow you to have fun every single second of the day.  No matter what you do or how much you love it, you still have to put in time doing tasks you don't enjoy as much, because those administrative tasks give you the access and ability to do the things you really want.  For example, if you're going to run a tourism company, you need to know how to calculate travel costs and figure out what is the lowest price you can offer to your customer while still making a profit.  Even if you just want to be a tour guide showing people around exciting locations, you will still need to know how to use a computer to access passenger lists and other crucial information.



This is your time to gain skills - you have the rest of your life to apply them in whatever setting you choose.



</answer>
