<original file>2.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk50435.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



Hayley
Member since: 31 March 2008
Total points: 377 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



What qualifications (A levels/Degrees) would be preferred/required to work in radio therapy or physiotherapy?



I'm doing AS's at the moment but interesting in careers from the mentioned fields.
Thanks!



</question>



<answer from>



The Open University
Member since: 26 September 2007



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



Hello
For details about relevant qualifications, have a look at both occupational areas within the Connexions website at http://www.connexions-direct.com/jobs4u/...



Radiography is split into two career pathways, diagnostic and therapeutic.  As diagnostic radiographers need a degree in diagnostic radiography and therapeutic radiographers need a degree in radiotherapy, applicants must be clear which pathway they wish to pursue before applying for courses.



Entry requirements for both degree courses usually include at least two A levels plus five GCSEs/S grades (A-C/1-3). A range of A level subjects is acceptable, although a science subject is often required; GSCEs often need to include English, maths and science.  Other qualifications, including relevant AS levels, may be accepted, either on their own or in combination with A levels.  For a recognised two year postgraduate course, entry is with a degree (often at least an upper second) in a science or health-related subject.  Applicants are generally expected to have observed radiography in practice, must be medically fit for the role and will be vetted to ensure that they are suitable persons to work with children and vulnerable adults.



For physiotherapy, entry requirements are very similar although usually three A’ levels (at high grades), including at least one science, are required.  Training involves a recognised three or four year university-based course, leading to a BSc in physiotherapy.  It’s also possible to complete a two year postgraduate MSc for those who have graduated in other relevant disciplines.



As exact entry requirements may vary between courses in both professional areas, it’s important to carefully check out details of available courses and entry requirements at http://ucas.com/students/coursesearch/.  Contact the relevant admissions officer for advice if you have any queries.



You might also find these sites useful for information on both careers:



http://www.nhscareers.nhs.uk/ - see the radiography and physiotherapy profiles on the ‘Careers A – Z’ section.



http://www.radiographycareers.co.uk/ - The Society of Radiographers’ website.
http://www.csp.org.uk/  - The Chartered Society of Physiotherapy website



I do hope this information helps with your career plans.



</answer>

<question>



Is it okay for me to not get good grades in high school since I'm going to be an entrepreneur?



I don't want to go to college. You probably think I'm a slacker student who is trying to take an easy way out, but I'm actually the top 10% of my school.



I really think the stuff I learn in high school will not help me in my career of owning my own icecream fast food chain when i'm older. College seems like a waste of time and money. I just want to get out there and do it.



Am I making a big mistake?



I missed school today because I got in a fight with my parents this morning. I used to feel horrible about missing school, but today it didnt bother me because I have a direction in life now.



I would love to hear advice from you.



</question>



<additional details>



A College education would definately help me run a business... but I don't need it.



Also, if my business fails I would try again and again.



Isn't that what entrepreneurship is about?



</additional details>



<answer from>



Mavisthe...
Member since: 02 August 2008
Total points: 12092 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Other - Careers & Employment



Homework Help



Books & Authors



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Missing a day of school won't hurt you. No need to be perfect; no need to feel bothered by it.



Keep the grades up, because:
1. The more you learn and know, the more it will help you down the line. It's not just for college. It comes in handy at the strangest times.
2. You never know, you may change your mind about college. Best to keep the option open.
3. Your parents will be happier and easier to live with if you do.



No, you don't need college to be an entrepreneur, though it does help to have some business knowledge.



Here's my advice:



You have a business in mind. Start working on it right now. Don't let your studies go, but in your spare time:
--- Start designing your business.



--- Research how to run a business, how to write a business plan.
--- Learn all about ice cream and the industry. What ice cream fast food chains have failed, and why? What will you do differently so yours won't fail?
--- Do you want to buy a franchise or create your own? Research that.
--- Get a part-time or summer job working at an ice cream fast food place.



--- Figure out how you'll pay for it and when it will start paying you back. Start saving now.
--- Figure out when you will open your first place or what steps you'll take between graduating from high school and opening.



You can write school papers and do projects related to this for some classes.



</answer>

Hopefully with a higher paying job you get after college.

<question from>



nick i
Member since: 04 September 2008
Total points: 96 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



College essay help!!!!?



I am not a strong writer and need help making my essay better. Also i would like to put in my experience of the first time i saw snow in the essay. Please be honest it will only help me out. I numbered the paragraphs.



1. Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. once said, “Faith is taking the first step even when you don't see the whole staircase.” As a young boy I did not completely grasp the sacrifice that my parents made for me in order to have a better life. What would it take for someone to give up everything they know; to risk never being with or seeing family or friends again? When I asked my parents how they were able to do this they responded with one word, “faith.” Faith was guiding them to a new foreign country they knew nothing about. With no promise of success they made the journey for that one chance to live the American dream.
2. In 1995 my family and I emigrated from Ecuador to the United States. My parents were in their last year of law school and had already established a way of life. They wanted to move to America in order to have better opportunities and to give their son a better life. The move was full of hardships, but with every obstacle we overcame I gained a better self awareness of the person I am today.



3. In the plane that would bring us to our new home was where my first obstacle presented itself. Communication is something most people take for granted in their everyday lives. But, on the plane that day I was not even able to tell the stewards what I wanted to drink or eat. Learning a new language proved to be a struggle. My parents depended on me to be able to translate since I was younger and could pick up a new language easier then they could. Day after day I pushed myself to learn as much as I could with out giving up until I had mastered the English language. It is this determination that has been instilled in me since I was a child that drives me to accomplish any feat that I may encounter in life. The hard work I put into learning the language also came from my parents.



4. From the first day we arrived my parents had to start working in order to feed the family. My dad worked at Dunkin Donuts in the morning, washed dishes in the afternoon at a small café, and at nights bussed tables at a restaurant. My mom also worked hard cleaning houses. After a couple of years, all their work finally paid of. My parents saved enough money to buy that small café where my dad started out washing dishes. Over the years I have seen the hard work of my parents open up opportunities that we thought would never be possible. Their work ethics are reflected in everything I do in life.



5. The work and sacrifices of my parents have truly changed my life. A memory that I will always have is returning to Ecuador for the first time nine years after I left. One of the first places my parents took me to visit was my old house. As I opened the door and looked around the incredibly small house I had an epiphany, I realized how far we had really come, how much our sacrifices and hard work had paid off. After seeing where I came from and what I have become, I now know that no dream is impossible to pursue. To most the American Dream is to become rich and famous. I am neither of the two, but still I feel like I have been able to attain it. To me having the possibility to become anything I want is the true American dream. The United States has given me the opportunity I need and my parents have provided me with the character I need to succeed.



</question>



<answer from>



preceden...
Member since: 18 February 2008
Total points: 889 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I'd make small grammatical changes like:



"Their decision entailed hardships..." in paragraph 2.



Also, it's very important that you not only illustrate how far your family has come, but also what you hope to do in the future. What career do you want to pursue? How will you use higher education to help the larger community? You make it very clear that you overcame numerous obstacles and risks, but you should make it even more clear how that shaped YOU and YOUR accomplishments. You should give perhaps one specific example, rather than the two/three brief examples you stated.



Writing a personal statement can be difficult. I like yours because it has a VOICE. A specific example would definitely make this personal statement a winner.



Good luck! :]



One more thing...
It'd be AWESOME if you could "tie up" the essay in the conclusion by saying something relating tot hat MLK Jr. quote you wrote in the introduction. You should say something like, "The staircase is there but I'm the one that will choose to take the next step." But use your own voice so it doesn't sound cheesy...it'll sound dope in the end and make a stronger impression on the readers that will be reading HUNDREDS of these a day! :D



</answer>



<asker's comment>



very helpful



</asker's comment>

<answer>

<answer from>



Maryn Bittner
Member since: 20 May 2007
Total points: 70770 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Books & Authors



</answer from>



<answer>



What you can do is compare yourself to the students they did accept. This is how the middle 50% of accepted students scored:
SAT - Critical Reading Middle 50%:	600-700 (you're 560)
SAT - Math Middle 50%:	610-710 (you're 610)
TPR Projected Range SAT Writing:	660-730 (you're 640)
Average High School GPA:	3.60 (you're 3.7)



Remember, a full quarter of accepted students scored lower than this.



I'd say you have a chance, but not a really good one. Your test scores are low, your GPA acceptable but not wonderful. Your activities help. An essay that's better than okay would help, too.



Should you even bother? It's up to you. Applying is not free, and you may well be rejected. But you won't ever know if you don't try.



</answer>

josh b
Member since: 09 May 2008

<answer>



Vocational usually refers to some sort of trade education.  That is, not academic.  In the case of a portfolio class, it would refer to technical skills - such as painting, film developing, drawing, etc. - as opposed to theoretical skills.

<question from>



VIGNESH A S
Member since: 30 May 2008
Total points: 123 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Which syllabus does Raffles Institution follow ?



it is located in singapore.



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



The RP offers a seamless curriculum from Secondary 1 to Junior College, culminating in the Singapore-Cambridge GCE ‘A’ Levels Examinations. Since you no longer need to prepare for the ‘O’ Levels Examinations, much of your precious time will be freed up to hone the valuable skills and habits of mind you will need to surge far ahead in life.



The rigorous, yet stimulating, RP is differentiated to cater to the needs of our gifted and talented students. It prepares you for the demands of a fast-changing world where you will be expected to be responsible risk-takers when leading and serving the community and nation.



The RP is modeled on exemplary gifted education principles. The curriculum is based on the Integrated Curriculum Model (Joyce Van Tassel-Baska, 1986), developed for high-ability learners. The key elements of the curriculum are advanced content, higher order process-and-product work and overarching themes and concepts. Socio-economic learning is a key thrust of the RP curriculum.



What you can look forward to in the RP
The RP offers multiple options for research and independent learning. If conducting cutting-edge research is your cup of tea, you can look to dedicated faculty members from institutes of higher learning and industry partners for mentorship.



Character and Leadership Education is a cornerstone of the RP. The cultivation of national consciousness and the development of personal effectiveness and leadership competencies are undertaken in such a way that your experience is always authentic and of practical value.
The Raffles Philosophy course is also offered to all students and aims to nurture a community of inquirers with strong empathy and well-honed reasoning and dialoguing skills.



Source(s):



http://www.raffles.sg/programme.html



</answer>

<question from>



mhm
Member since: 10 February 2007
Total points: 144 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Where will I get in: UCSD, UCLA, UCB, Johns Hopkins, or Emory?



-around a 4.0 weighted gpa (calculated using the UC system of grading)
-6 year volleyball player, 3 year club volleyball
-cross country team at school for 1 year
-newspaper member for last 2 years
-2 APs, 3 Honors classes sophomore year
-4 APs, 1 Honors class JR year
-highest practice sat score in one sitting= 720 critical reading, 680 math, 700 writing (2100 T)
-highest individual practice scores= 720 critical reading, 700 math, 710 writing (2130 T)
-taking an sat class, goal is to get 2250 on real test
-199 on psat w/out studying
-choir member freshman year
-volunteered at a children's day camp, hospital, habitat for humanity (200+ hours total)
-going on immersion trip to india this summer
-won volunteer service award
-principal's honor roll entire high school career
-part of south asian student association
-math club
-csf, nhs
-ca resident
-attending a college-prep private high school



Any feedback about any of htese schools would be appreciated! Thank you!



</question>



<answer from>



J M
Member since: 13 June 2007
Total points: 1404 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



You sound like you'd have a decent shot at any of them, but college admissions can be very unpredictable one year to the next.



What you have to say in application essays can often matter a *great* deal.



If there's something that makes you unique or rare for a given school any given year, that can matter too. For example, Johns Hopkins might rarely have volleyball players apply this year, but UCLA might be flooded, or vice versa. Schools tend to like a good mix of who attends and try to strategize for it and most of that is entirely out of your control. It's important to simply be as honest as possible on your applications so you end up where something is truly a good "fit" for you, not just someplace that you think might impress others but ends up making you miserable.



Sometimes a school closest to where you currently live is more likely to take you, sometimes they say college is for learning and you need "diversity" in your life so you should go elsewhere.



Sometimes a school puts a lot of weight on test scores over anything, sometimes they really want extracurriculars, sometimes they want original self-starters, sometimes they say they have too many candidates already from your high school, etc., etc.... and it can even often vary one year to the next where their values are and why.



Anyway, your essays and writing (and recommendations) can shade a lot of how everything else gets interpreted.



Unpredictable.



</answer>



<answer from>



Priyanka
Member since: 06 October 2008
Total points: 141 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



College-University
at
http://collegeuniversity1.blogspot.com/



</answer>

<original file>11564.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk61063.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



krislove
Member since: 31 October 2007
Total points: 137 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Is it worth it to get a medical coding certificate?



I am thinking of going back to school, and i want to get a certificate for a better job- I've heard medical coding was the wave of the future?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



I am a recruiter for the medical field and every day people come to me with a medical biling and coding certificate looking for work. Here is the problem, no one wants to hire someone without experience. It's really a tough place to be. It's not a position that medical esabilishments want to take a chance on training someone on the job because one mistake takes hours to correct and can cost the practice $$$. In my 4 years here, I have never seen one of our clients hire someone out of school with an externship.



In addition, its a really boring job....maybe you could job shadow someone one day before you decide to go to school



Source(s):



Experience in the industry.



</answer>

<original file>12012.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk61562.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



Mercy
Member since: 21 June 2008
Total points: 77 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Three year BA or Four Year Honors BA?



I am currently doing a four year honors bachelor of arts and am trying to determine whether I should continue with it or if I should switch over to a three year which would save me a year of time and tuition. What are the long term career benefits of a four year honors over a three year general Bachelors degree?



</question>



<answer from>



Skye E
Member since: 27 September 2008
Total points: 220 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Depends on your chosen field, but as an employer who interviews college grads for jobs, I can tell you that we totally don't care.  1) A future employer will probably not notice.  They want to see the degree has been earned, end of story.  They totally don't care about how/when.  2) There are usually no employer/interview brownie points for "honors," and that can even be a red flag--hyper-successful college kids sort of have a reputation for being lousy employees in the beginning (no teamwork skills, it's all about "them.")



Some professions care about which school...some don't.  Save yourself the money, just get the degree.  And don't under-estimate the importance of going on for a Master's Degree.  That's a more worthy goal than a BA/BS with honors.



</answer>



<answer from>



Riya
Member since: 04 June 2007
Total points: 58 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



online degree-



http://tntcarernet.com/



</answer>



<answer from>



Kitten
Member since: 28 September 2006
Total points: 875 (Level 2)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



I'd vote for the 3 year program. Essentially, once you graduate few employers will even ask about your gpa, transcripts, or your program. In fact in the 15 years I've worked all over the world only when I worked for the Federal Gov't did they care about my transcripts and that was for pay scale placement. Save the money and the time, get your BA early and sink that saved money into a graduate program if that's something you'd like to pursue or start work early to get a head start on paying off any loans or college debt. Ultimately, you will not be hurt at all by doing the 3year program, especially if the only thing you get for the 4year program is a star on your degree, save that extra money and get out of there!



</answer>

<question>



What do and don't you use from high school in college?



The things you learned in High school,
How much of it will you use or not use?



</question>



<answer from>



fexii
Member since: 25 April 2007
Total points: 508 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



That depends on the classes you took in high school, what college you go to and what your major will be in college. In general, you can assume that you'll use a ton of the stuff you learned in classes you're interested in, and not so much from classes you're disinterested in. College lets you lead your own path much more than high school.



</answer>



<answer from>



Tiel123
Member since: 21 April 2008
Total points: 334 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Depends what subject - but what i found is that the first few weeks - up to 2 months..usually its just refreshing the memory period where you look at things you learned in the subject in high school as a whole. Then once everyone has grasped the basis - you go on progressing, however things do ocassionally pop up from high school.



For example if you learned the times tables in school, these will be needed in college as they are the basis to some of the problems you may need to solve in maths.



</answer>



<answer from>



Skye E
Member since: 27 September 2008
Total points: 220 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



You DON'T use the silly social stuff...prom?  Fun, but hardly a skill used in college.  Good social skills are a must, but social skills in college are based more in studying together (even if you're studying over a beer.)  Being able to contribute intelligently to a group project by knowing your stuff is more important that "looking cool."  High School academic skills are a starting point, but your college professors won't be impressed by high school grades.  They only look at how you're doing in THEIR class--can you keep up, are you able to build on your learning.  My experience was that I didn't need the high school sense of cliques and fashion, but I totally needed every ounce of academic skill I had--and then some.  You will use everything academic from high school, but college will demand you use it better and learn even more about it. Writing, math, PC skills, logic, reasoning, reading comprehension...all of it at a very high level.  Of course, all this depends on your major...but be prepared for the basic ed classes to move fast and have high expectations.  Good luck!



</answer>



<answer from>



The_Magi...
Member since: 14 May 2006
Total points: 24172 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Contrary to what a  lot of people on here might think, I've actually used a fair amount of what I learned in high school in my college classes (and I'm only a first semester freshman).



</answer>

<question>



How can you apply for Medicine without UKCAT?



I missed the registration deadline for UKCAT and now i am at a bit of a dead end. Is there anyway you can apply for Medicine and do the test at a later date? Or can you start a related course like Biology or Chemistry and swap to Medicine in your second year?



</question>



<answer from>



ZrZoOoR
Member since: 30 September 2008

<answer>



i dont no

<answer from>



JB
Member since: 11 August 2008
Total points: 348 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Hey, if you want to study at one of the universities which require the UKCAT, then I'm afraid you would have to go next year - it's not possible to switch courses in the second year as the subject matter you would have covered would be different. No degree courses allow you to do that. You also definitely need to do the test before applying. However - have you considered applying to one of the universities which don't require the UKCAT? They include some fairly prestigious universities eg Bristol, Birmingham, Liverpool, and probably more that I'm unaware of. If I were you I'd do some research into it as it would be a shame to have to wait a year because of one missed deadline. Good luck!



</answer>



<answer from>



CattyOne
Member since: 04 October 2006
Total points: 1195 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



You can go for the Braford Clinical sciences course, then 2nd yr go to leeds for medicine (if you make it, that is).
Leeds, King's, Georges, Southampton, UEA - have 6 yr "conversion" courses but you usually have to have AAA in subjects other than science i.e. for those who only just realised they really want to do medicine and just picked the wrong subjects. Or Some of them don't accept A Level students though, so have a look at the criteria on their websites.
King's have a 6 yr course which accepts A Levels but you have to live in one of the boroughs listed on their website. (I'm not sure if you need UKCAT for these, but I know King's run their own test anyway so don't know if they would use it)
Some unis in the past didn't want UKCAT but not sure now



</answer>

<answer from>

Member since: 05 February 2008

<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



eri
Member since: 06 October 2006
Total points: 91335 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Astronomy & Space



Higher Education (University +)



Other - Education



</answer from>



<answer>



A thesis is a longer research paper, usually done by students seeking to graduate from college with honors.  Not everyone will write one; most people won't actually.  It's supposed to be as a result of working on a research project with an adviser in your department, and may be original work in some cases - in addition to a thesis, you could present the findings at a conference or contribute to a paper.



</answer>

<question from>



sketch33...
Member since: 16 April 2007
Total points: 140 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Community College to Engineering Program?



Hi.



My current plans are to become a pharmacist, but I might change that. I am in my first year of community college in North Carolina. I am currently taking intermediate algebra and won't be able to take Calculus until my last semester of my second year. If I were to become an engineer then I would probably transfer to UNCC. Correct me if i'm wrong, but it seems as if it is necessary to have Calculus 1-3 + differential equations before going into their engineering program. How would this work out for me so I can take 2 years here and transfer?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



I would like to say that if you aren't already in calculus your freshman year of college, especially at a community college (much, much easier than state or private school), that you should enroll as soon as possible or re-evaluate your dreams.



I am on the same route to become a pharmacist and I know from experience that one needs an extensive background in chemistry and mathematics. Engineering is based on mathematics and physics.



If you cannot take or handle calculus, I would suggest that you find alternate plans to your career path, at least from engineering. I took intermediate algebra as a sophomore in high school (up to calculus 2 in high school, all passed with "A"s) and struggled with the higher level math classes during undergraduate. I passed them with good grades, but I would suggest if you are planning to become one or the other to step up your classwork.



</answer>

<question>



To be a writer do you need a english degree?



Would i need that if i wanted to work for a magazine or newspaper? Could i just minor in english?



</question>



<additional details>



at my school journalism is an emphasis area under english.



</additional details>



<answer from>



CoachT
Member since: 24 January 2007
Total points: 31691 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



A degree in English is really about reading and analyzing literature and not so much about writing - except writing about literature.



To be a writer you'll really want a degree in a specialized topic you plan to write about or a generalist degree in either journalism or communication.  At some schools the journalism and technical writing tracks are a part of the English degree - at most they are separate.



Magazine and newspaper writing are not the same unless the magazine is the likes of Newsweek.  Magazine writers tend to have a special area of expertise such as finance or fashion and they are more expert in those areas than in writing.



</answer>

<question>



What is the differece between full time Ph.D and part time P.hD.?



I am doing M.Tech in computer science. I want to do P.hD. Which one to do fulltime or parttime. I mean which one has better career oppurtunities?



</question>



<answer from>



sng998
Member since: 19 May 2008
Total points: 310 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



I don't know about career opportunities, but I am also looking into PhD programs at the moment and I have found many programs only want full-time students.  I found that several off the brochures and web pages I have looked at distinctly say that only full-time students apply.
In my opinion, full-time would offer you more opportunities for free education, assistanceships, fellowships, living stipends, etc...
And you would be done twice as fast!



According to money magazine (May 2006) both college professor and computer/IT analyst are the in the top 7 most desirable jobs and the most needed in the next 10 years!  So you have a lot to look forward to.  Good Luck!



</answer>



<answer from>



eri
Member since: 06 October 2006
Total points: 91337 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Astronomy & Space



Higher Education (University +)



Other - Education



</answer from>



<answer>



Both of them give you a PhD in the end, the only difference is how long it takes you to finish it - you'll finish a full-time PhD much faster than part-time (fewer credits a semester).



</answer>

<question>



Why tution of University of Texas at Dallas is more expensive than University of North Texas Denton?



Why the tution of University of Texas at Dallas is more expensive than University of North Texas Denton? i alway thought UNT is a better school compare to UTD; I mean the tution difference for 1 semester is almost 1000 dollar difference;



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



You are paying to be part of the UT system at university of Texas at Dallas.  UT system is one of the biggest and more prestigious of state institutions in the nation and you pay for that name... Similarly you would pay more to go to UC (california) that to go to another university in the state.



</answer>

<question>



English, Finance, Confidence?



i am an international business student studying in Sg. I want to improve my English, Finance Knowledge and do at least "not bad" in my presentation. I also kinda lost my confidence during the period because i dont' speak English well and I look bad and fat for a business student. Any ideas how to raise my confidence up and improve my English??



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Are you in an English speaking country right now? If not, you should try to do an exchange program. Immersing yourself in the language is the best way to improve. I you are in an English speaking country, perhaps you could get a language coach (or at least someone that is willing to have lots of conversations with you). Or you could try watching a lot of tv or joining a group where you have to discuss things (like a book club?).



</answer>

<question>



Edit please summary of book 10 points!?



Please edit my summary and give some tips! Thanks in advance! It's about 441 words and I need to make it 250-300 words.



The story starts with Canada accepting over one thousand teens that have lost their families during the tragic events of the World War II. After required paperwork, they start a new life with their host families across the country. The youth lived atrocious adult lives during the war, but as refugees they were again considered as children. Amongst the many orphans were Lilli, Danny and many of their friends. Lilli was an Auschwitz survivor who saw her mother and sister walk to their death. Her older sister was sent to Palestine. The other orphans whom Lilli met on the ship to Canada were Kurt, Sophie, Sylvie, Max and Danny of whom all ended up in the city of Vancouver to their new families. Lilli was assigned to the Chandler family but could not adjust due to Mrs. Chandler’s arrogance, and after an episode at school, is ordered to move out by her. At her next house, she must do cheap labour and baby-sit at the Davidson. Her desire to go to school and progress are put in danger. Whilst Lilly deals with hard times, she is comforted when the other refugees meet regularly and discuss their experiences. Danny culls to be independent and halt his education until he can provide for himself. Lilli finds friendship which was something she dearly missed, Marilyn, a Canadian-born. Marilyn and her parents expected a Jewish boy-orphan; however, it was delayed to another shipment of orphans. Marilyn does not understand why “the greenies” are not trying to fit in, yet she shows a little more compassion than her class mates. Marilyn and Lilli get suspended from school when they rally together amongst other girls. This leads the Mrs. Davidson to order Lilli to move out. Lilli’s social worker finds Lilli a new place to stay where she must take care of an elderly man. Lilli is offered a week to move out, and she keeps it a secret to Marilyn and her friends. As Marilyn gets to know Lilli and her orphan friends, she invites them for dinner. However, Lilli spoils the dinner by announcing that she is moving out of the Davidsons. Marilyn and her parents have a talk and decide that Lilli could stay with them. At last, for many years, she feels loved and home again.



</question>

<question>



Should I transfer or should I stick it out? I am a junior now and very lost?



Hello I need Help



I just transferred to a new college from a community college, I thought this was the school for me, but now that I think about it more I think it was really my ex-girlfriend who wanted me to come here (we just broke up a month ago) my classes are hard as **** and I have failed all my first test really badly. My parents tell me that I am allowed to come home and go to a different school next semester and I have been thinking about doing that. I love the people here and the school is amazing, I don't like the way the Prof want to screw you over ( they say "The test won't be easy if you study a lot and read the notes and study more you may be able to pass with a 70". I always thought learning should be fun, if I do transfer I will be going to a lower rank school, but I will be home and I do miss my family a lot.



My question is, should I stick it out and just stress over everything for the next two years or should I follow what I feel is right and leave next semester and go to a school where I know I can do a better job at and not stress or pull all nighters trying to get my work done.



Please help me, I don't know what to do and my parents tell me to do what I want, but they thing is if I leave I will feel like a failure, but if I don't leave I am afraid I will fail out.



</question>



<answer from>



opiniono...
Member since: 31 August 2008
Total points: 1122 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



It sounds as if you have a lot to deal with right now.  How much of your situation is due to breaking up with your gf?  Some thing like that can really mess with your head in general; if it happens shortly after transferring to a new school, a person can feel angry, depressed or unhappy...all of which can affect your focus and ability to concentrate.  Add in that you miss your family and it sounds as if you have a lot of personal stress.



You wrote that you love the people and the school is amazing.  Why don't you try this for the current semester?



Talk to the learning or academic support office to learn how to learn at your new school.  Maybe part of the problem is just not studying correctly. Take advantage of any workshops they have on adjusting to their school, study tips and strategies...anything really that will give you a better sense of how the Uni community operates.



Talk to your professors if you are unclear about course expectations. If you have TAs, talk to them. Find study groups or a study buddy so you can collaborate.  Try to find people in your program who have already taken courses with your profs. Some faculty are just jerks when they toss out the sort of comments made to your class, so find out exactly what they want on their tests.



If you find yourself stressing over your break up, take advantage of the counseling services your school offers.  Breakups happen a lot during college and maybe talking to an objective outsider will help...I don't think it will hurt. They are familar with all the stressors you are dealing with right now. Almost everyone has adjustments issues on some level and they can help.



I encourage you to stick out the semester and see how it goes.  You might find that as your life balances out, you have a better sense of what your profs want, and when you are more confident in your abilities to succeed there, that you won't want to leave. However, if it still feels 'right' at the end of semester and you want to leave, then do it.



It's great that your parents are supportive. If you do decide to leave, I'd like you to think about this  ...realizing that a school is not a good fit does not mean you are a failure. It just means that it didn't work out the way you originally thought it would. Think about the larger lessons you are learning as a result of this. You are right, learning should be fun - don't spend the next two years agonizing about school.  IT'S JUST NOT WORTH IT ! ! !  Unless you would transfer into a school that is so bad it shouldn't even be open, don't worry about the lesser rank.



Good luck to you.  I wish you well.



Source(s):



25 years higher education admission and advising administration; faculty.



</answer>

</asker's comment>

If you want to know about the club, ask around to Spanish teachers about when the meetings are or who the officers of the club are so you can ask them. You can't just sign up and say that you were in them, because not only is that dishonest but the colleges will usually check up on them if they are unsure.



Also, and this maybe a little late to say, but colleges don't like it when you join a bunch of clubs your junior year. That shows them that you are only thinking about college when joining these clubs and are not really interested. They like to see it when you are in clubs for years, starting Freshman year preferably. But try to actually attend and go for an officer position if you're really thinking about college. It shows that you like to take charge and be a leader.

<answer from>



embroidery fan

<answer>



Usually people turn in the bibliography along with the rough draft. Your teacher is smart, though.  The teacher wants to know if you did your research, first.  That's OK.



Just write out your bibliography in the format you've been assigned.



The old-fashioned way I learned was:



Albert, Jane. Research is Fun. (NY: Research Press, 1971).
Allred, Robert. Having Fun with Research. (Albany, NY: Beecher Press, Inc., 2008).



etc.



Source(s):



I have a Master's degree, plus a degree in Education.



</answer>



<answer from>



Shiori_h...
Member since: 06 August 2008
Total points: 2402 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



Yeah, like others have said, there's nothing at all unusual about this kind of assignment. Professors will check it to see if you're actually working on your paper, and many will also make sure you're not missing an important source or using something that's been debunked. It kind of depends on the professor, but that's what most of mine did when they asked my classmates and I to turn bibliographies in.



Unfortunately I can't help you much with what your teacher wants on the bibliography other than a list of references you're using (by the way, save the bibliography you write now so you just have to edit it later instead of retyping it all for your paper). If your teacher mentioned anything about annotation, then you need a write a couple sentences about each source, listing off the main things it covers and how you think it will be useful (e.g. "This source discusses X theory and defines terms A, B, and C. In addition, it has some pictures and graphs that will help me illustrate my point." or something along those lines). Check over your syllabus, the professor might have written something about that in the syllabus.



My only other recommendation to you is to make sure you get clarification on these things from the teacher a few days before projects are due so you're not scrambling around like this again. It's kind of tough to give you anything more than really general advice without knowing more details about what the teacher said about the assignment. Good luck with the bibliography, though.



</answer>



<answer from>



Ultimate Nobody
Member since: 10 May 2008
Total points: 145 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>

its a in-depth book that shows many ways to format your information.  check it out if your library has it, i bought it off amazon for about $20.

<question>



What is the minimum age of getting a scholarship?



</question>



<answer from>



embroidery fan
Member since: 07 May 2007
Total points: 21858 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



You can probably get a scholarship at any age, if you are capable of going to college.  For example, if you are gifted, and are 14 or 15 but are capable of enrolling in college and have been admitted to one, it's hard to imagine that the scholarship foundations would not accept your application.  Just get your principal, counselor, home school teacher, or the registrar of the college which has accepted you to write you a special accompanying letter, if that may be needed.



</answer>



<answer from>



Shiori_h...
Member since: 06 August 2008
Total points: 2404 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



It really depends on the scholarship. Some don't have an age restriction as much as a year-in-college restriction (e.g. you can only get a particular scholarship if you're a freshman/sophomore/junior/senior). Scholarships are usually very good at listing off every single thing that would qualify you for consideration for the scholarship as well as the things that would automatically disqualify you, so check the requirements for each scholarship that interests you. Many schools also have scholarships they give to some students, and I'm pretty sure most of them are free of age restrictions -- I got scholarships like those all through my college career and I was usually 1-2 years younger than most of the other students in my year who got the same scholarships, so I'm pretty sure there were no age restrictions in those cases. But again, you'll want to double-check with the individual schools and make sure.



</answer>

<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



Gilbert
Member since: 10 December 2006
Total points: 312 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Has anyone attended a top law school while simultaneously obtaining a MBA online from a reputable school?



Here's what I'm proposing. Get into a top-20 law school, only work summers (as usual), and take accelerated online MBA classes from a decent school starting in your second (2L) year (first year is just too much).



ASU, for example, has a great online MBA program where you can finish in two years by taking a series of six-week-long classes. You only take one class at a time, so in theory, if you are taking a full law school load it's really only equivalent to adding one more class.



The benefit, of course, is you finish a year earlier than even combined JD/MBA programs. Additionally, you get to take a wider range of electives in law school, not necessarily limited to those that double-count. Also, you don't fall into the "man (person) without a country" syndrome that many complain about, where you become close to fellow 1L's, only to leave them the next year in lieu of starting the MBA program. Then, when you're a 2L in law school (third year), you're kind of caught in between all your former 3L friends and your new 2L acquaintances. Finally, if you pull it off successfully, you will REALLY set yourself apart from your peers in the job market.



Of course, there are great risks. Obvious ones are grades. Sure, if you pull it off, it's great; but lowering your grades is simply not worth it. Also, some employers might view you as impatient, too much of a risk taker, and someone who isn't clear on what he wants to do (business or law)...



I tailor this question to top law school students. Then, I can see this being really to the advantage of students in second-tier law schools who are trying to get into the same big firms (500+, $160K) as those who went to top schools. The school itself won't necessarily be any easier (law school is tough no matter how you slice it), but the benefits are even greater.



So what does the community think?



</question>

<current file name>yqa_uk71990.txt</current file name>




<category>

<question from>

<original file>6644.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk71999.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



K K
Member since: 03 December 2006
Total points: 6075 (Level 5)



</question from>



<question>



Is it possible to withdraw my applications?



Is it possible to withdraw an application to a school almost immediately after you submitted it? I submitted two applications online yesterday, and then realized that I made somewhat large mistakes on the applications! I am going to talk to my college counselor at school tomorrow, but I am very worried about it and was wondering if there is any way to withdraw these applications and then re-apply later. Please help! Best answer gets 10 points!



</question>



<answer from>



opiniono...
Member since: 31 August 2008
Total points: 1122 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



You can't technically 'withdraw' an application because when a school enters it into their computer system, it will generate a student ID number that is uniquely yours...and filing on line results in an immediate ID generation. That number will always be associated only with you, so when you re-apply, your new application will be added on to applicant file that was originally created with your first applications. The only way this wouldn't happen is if one of the errors was a wrong social security number, but admission offices have ways to match those errors in the future.



I'm not sure from your question if you sent two applications to the same school or sent applications with mistakes to two different schools. If you sent two apps to the same school, all we do is merge the two applications into one and that's it!  That is not a problem for us at all. If you applied to two schools, just follow up with both of them.



(That's just a bit of info about how admission offices work, which may be more than you need/want to know but hopefully it helps to understand what happens when your application is received.)



Now, your real question is can you withdraw your applications?  ABSOLUTELY...you are already correct to contact them immediately.  Explain what happened and maybe you can have your application fees refunded (if you had to pay them.)



You don't explain what the large mistakes were, but if they involved something like listing schools incorrectly or other factual information, the admission office may be able to correct those without you having to reapply.  Those can be corrected by changing data that downloaded from the on-line application.



If your mistake was something like sending in a draft essay with mistakes in it instead of the final, a new application allows you to send in a new one but they may allow you to submit a corrected copy.



Explain what happened. Admission offices are pretty good at assessing situations and really do want to work with applicants who make mistakes.  You sound very sincere and very worried. I suspect they will work with you.



Good luck to you.  You will be fine.



Source(s):



25 years higher ed administration in admissions and advising; faculty



</answer>



<asker's comment>

</asker's comment>

</category>



<question from>



Tia Maria
Member since: 02 November 2007
Total points: 234 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Law Degree, Part-Time or Distance Learning, which would you recommend?



I am going to do a Law degree next year, however I am unsure whether it would be better doing it part-time or distance learning.  I have previous experience in a law degree which I did not complete. And I followed that by studying a Diplom in Law distance learning.  However, I have not combined the 2 before.



Can anyone with experience in doing a Law degree either part-time or distance learning give me some insight what to expect.  I will have to work so these are the only 2 options.



</question>



<answer from>



Grim C
Member since: 26 September 2008
Total points: 122 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



i think it would be very difficult to do a law degree without some tutor input.
One of my close friends did hers part time and began a new career as a result.
Some employers will also want to see that you have taken part in events, such as moots, which are not part of a distance learning programme



</answer>



<answer from>



Phill
Member since: 02 September 2006
Total points: 811 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



My advice is to do the course Part time, Whilst distance learning is equally as hard, it is looked down on by lawyers,



In terms of work content, you will have your seven core subjects over the 4-5 yrs dependent on how long your part time course is, plus your electives.



Your main workload will be the associated reading, of case law, academic text. Law is very personal study driven, so you get what you put in it, but expect to be doing 16-20 hrs personal reading.



having studied a degree and diploma prior to this your well ahead of the game, as you know most of the work is surrounding assignment, and research techniques.



I don't foresee you running into problems in the first year balancing it with work, but as you know law is not the easiest of subjects and takes time and dedication.



I wish you luck, just don't burn yourself out



</answer>

Education & Reference

</category>



<question from>



sad girl
Member since: 06 October 2008
Total points: 81 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Was uk colonized by other?



was the UK colonized? if yes, when and by whome??



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



So far as recorded history is concerned, by the Romans in the 1st century AD. After they had left, successive waves of Germanic tribes settled and pushed the remaining Romanised Britons  back into Wales and the west. These colonists became known collectively as the Anglo Saxons. In the 9th 10th and 11th centuries AD there were raids by the Vikings who colonised the north and east of the country. When an accommodation was reached with them by King Alfred that area became known as the Danelaw. The in 1066 came the last and most long lasting colonisation when William the Conqueror defeated Harald, the last Saxon king and created an Norman-French over lordship



</answer>

This is because once u start a career in management, you may not be sure whether u will be able to continue in your field as you might be offered to work in different area like HR or something. In that case, i prefer doing an MS or MTech. But if you are so adamant about doing an MBA, you may try specializations like operations and system(nowadays most universities offer dual specializations for MBA), both of which are relevant to the technical graduation you have earned.



</answer>

<question>



Community College Transfers please.?



How many of you have transfered in 2 years or less... I'm really trying to live in a dorm and get out of this damn house already. I'm taking all the necessary classes right now. English 1A, Pre Cal, Sociology, psychology



</question>



<answer from>



Peace233
Member since: 08 October 2007
Total points: 260 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



I went to a community college for two years and now I have transfered and am a junior at my new university.



The transfer process is not difficult, so long as you know the admissions criteria for transfers at the school of your choice. Of course you will have to fill out the admissions application, and then you will have to submit an official copy of your transcripts.



But make sure that you get the bulk of your gen ed. classes out of the way, like the math, science and English courses. Don't worry about the classes that are geared toward a specific major: most colleges want you to start taking the degree specific courses AFTER you transfer.



Source(s):



I just transfered to a four year institution.



</answer>

<original file>224.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk82022.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



varialhe...
Member since: 16 June 2007
Total points: 238 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Requirements for going to med school?



i am planning to go to hopkins. i am just in eighth grade but i want to plan it out early. What are the requirements? do you have to be in band and stuff? because i play trumpet and i really dont like my school band. please tell me step by step such as required GPA, what classes i need to take and what i have to prepare for. thank you.



</question>



<answer from>



ThatGuyO...
Member since: 22 October 2008
Total points: 113 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Well before you go to Medical School, you have to finish up a few courses that compose up what is called "Pre-Medicine, or Pre-Med." You usually take your Pre-Med classes in your first two years of college, also known as your undergraduate years. During this time, the standard course set-up is:



1 Year of Biology with Lab
1 Year of General Chemistry
1 Year of Organic Chemistry
1 Year of Physics with Lab



Those classes are pretty much the backbone of Medicine. Other courses include Biochemistry, Neuroscience, Pharmacology, Biopsychology, Anatomy, Genetics from the top of my head.



Basically, just do well it high school to attend a good college. Medical school really only focuses on your College GPA and perhaps its prestige. So keep your fundamental sciences (Biology, Chemistry, Physics) strong, and you should do fine. Good for you for planning out your future early on.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



you explained well, flip did too.



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Flip
Member since: 06 September 2008
Total points: 520 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



First, graduate from high school. If you don't like band, don't do it. Do have some sort of extra-curricular that makes you happy.



As for GPA, that depends on where you want to go to college. Challenge yourself with honors classes, if you can. Obviously, the higher the GPA the better, but not at the expense of not ever doing anything other than studying/homework.
Talk to your school counselor about where/how to look at colleges.



Second, go to college. Get a B.S. or a B.A. degree. This usually takes four years. For medical school, your experiences at college are most important. You do not have to go to Hopkins for college to go there for medical school.



In college, talk to a pre-health/pre-med advisor for help scheduling the right classes and choosing a major. You'll need certain science classes, need to take certain exams etc.



Basically: All you need to focus on now is doing as well as you can and graduating from high school. In both high school and college contact the people who's entire job it is to get you into a good college (guidance counselor) and then into medical school (pre-health advisor in college).



Also: it's great that you are planning this so early, but also make sure you plan on some fun too. It is healthy and it will look good on applications.



Good luck.



</answer>

Member since: 01 December 2007

<answer>



You should include your senior year. I did this when first applying to college as I was advised to do so.

<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



chezzaaa...
Member since: 12 March 2008
Total points: 264 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Which University for BA Photography?!?



I am hoping to go to University in September 2009, to study a BA in Photography. I have narrowed my final choices down to (Not in order):



Southampton Solent.
Derby.
Brighton.
Falmouth.



Does anybody here study in any of these Universities? What is the location like? Is there a large social scene? Comments on the course/accommodation/finance?



Any help would be greatly appreciated!



</question>



<additional details>



This is in the UK, not the US :)



</additional details>



<answer from>



The Open University
Member since: 26 September 2007



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



Hello
Since you’ve narrowed down your university options, try http://www.unistats.com/  to compare information between the universities and their courses.  It will take a little time, as you need to register and then compile the list of universities for comparison, but it should prove to be a useful exercise.



Check the relevant university websites for information, including dates for open days, virtual tours or feedback from current and previous students.



It’s well worth visiting on an open day for the opportunity to meet staff and students, check out university facilities and find out about availability and cost of accommodation in the university area, particularly for first year.



If this isn’t possible, then consider contacting individual departments such as the faculty for detailed information regarding course content, the careers service for information on graduate employment prospects or the Students Union for information on facilities and student life in general.



Perhaps some current or previous students will also provide you with feedback.



I hope this helps with your query and wish you well in your future study.



s



</answer>

<original file>82.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk82346.txt</current file name>




<category>



Education & Reference



Higher Education (University +)



</category>



<question from>



Alexande...
Member since: 17 October 2008
Total points: 101 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What qualifications do I need to get admitted into MIT?



I want to get admitted in MIT. So I need to know what I need to do to get admitted there. Like what grades I need to get in my O and A Levels and if I need to do SAT or IELTS or any extra curriculum. Please help



</question>



<answer from>



Genome
Member since: 19 October 2007
Total points: 341 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Seriously?



Well, you need to have an outstanding GPA. MIT is one of the most selective school in this country, plus they have very strict admission 'quotas' based upon your specific region and type of school. Are you from the East Coast? Private school? You need a much higher GPA than your Southern state public school counterparts. It's also slightly easier if you're female.



That's not enough. You need a high GPA with honors courses, AP classes, or whatever the top tracks your school offers. You need a diverse number of extra curricular activities, and you need leadership positions. You need to show that you might be a math/science kid, but you also like history/english/whatever. Think angular, but interesting. Because everyone has high scores and was the president of their math club. Do you have awards? Did you do research over the summer? Are you a Seimans/Intel finalist?



Testing requirements: http://www.mitadmissions.org/topics/appl... . Short story? Math (1c or 2c) and Science. Think high scores. 750s or above, you know the drill. Check it out: http://collegesearch.collegeboard.com/ac...



I'm guessing from your question that you'd be an international student? Good. Very good. US schools like that. However, remember that applying to a school in the US means that your transcript becomes the most important thing in determining your admission. How are your exam scores? Teacher recommendations?



Frankly, MIT is a very difficult school to get into, even if you are extremely qualified. So do your absolute best, both in and out of school, and always present yourself as an interesting, diversified candidate.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thanks!



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



jfcruzr
Member since: 10 May 2008
Total points: 3927 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



4.0 gpa or some awesome extracurricular activities.



1200 SAT scores or some impressive work-study experience.
No matter what, you'll be competing with top applicants for MIT.



if you have none of the above and REALLY want to go to MIT, write them some kind of creative poem, sing them a song.  Do something to make them remember YOU and your desire to attend their college, stand out.



</answer>

annonymo...

<question>



Getting my masters degree?



I am a little confused on how I can do this. I have an associates degree in computer programming and will soon have a bachelors in Business Information Systems, but I want to get my masters. Does my masters have to be in line with my bachelors? I hear tons of people talking about an MBA(masters business administration?). I am just confused and want to get my masters in something that allows me to be a manager of IT personel and such Please let me know what school I should go to and what masters would be most beneficial. I live in Indianapolis, IN so I have butler, IUPUI, Indiana University, Purdue. I want the one that will help my career the most please.



</question>

derdakta...
Member since: 29 July 2008

Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



an MBA will require you to probably take certain prerequisites to get in.

</answer>



<answer from>



roaringm...
Member since: 12 December 2005
Total points: 41598 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Higher Education (University +)



</answer from>



<answer>



Your masters program doesn't necessarily need to be in the same field as your bachelors degree. In fact, MBAs are specifically designed for people who did NOT major in business as an undergraduate.



If you want to manage people in IT, you can either get an MS in a tech field like computer science, or an MBA. However, an MBA will make it more likely you'd manage people than an MS would. But either one could work.



Most MBA programs either require or strongly prefer you to have at least two years of full time work experience between your bachelors degree and applying to the MBA program. In addition, if you hope to get a job managing people in IT, employers, hiring out of MBA or MS programs, will require that you actually have worked in IT before you got your masters. So if you haven't already worked in IT, I strongly suggest you take a couple of years and work in the field, then apply to masters degree programs.



In addition, if you're lucky, your employer may offer tuition reimbursement, and will pay for all or part of your MBA. This can be a huge deal, since MBA programs tend to be very expensive.



If you decide to go for an MS in a tech field, your #1 choice must be Purdue. It's one of the best tech schools in the US. Your second choice could be Indiana U.



If an MBA, I'd have you look at those two schools as well, as your primary choices. But no matter where you go for your MBA, make sure it's AACSB accredited, or you may have difficulty with some employers coming out. That AACSB accreditation is key.



</answer>

<answer from>



davidand...

</answer from>



<answer>

</answer>



<answer from>



obsdabef...
Member since: 28 October 2006
Total points: 647 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Each day, spend at least one hour on each subject. Make note cards from your notes and memorize them. Go through chapters in your textbook and highlight the important parts. Make note cards on the stuff you highlighted and memorize those too.



Keep yourself hydrated and drink lots of water. Eat a banana and some dark chocolate. Those foods have shown to help boost your memorization skills.



Every night before you go to bed, make sure to skim through all your note cards. You are more likely to remember information that way.



Since note cards can't be used to remember everything, get a list of practice problems from each subject. Try to go through each one WITHOUT using your notes or textbook. It is the only way to get yourself to actually know how to do the problems on your own. Go back and check your answers and then attempt a fresh set of problems.



I find it also helps me on exams if I "teach" someone else the material. Snag an empty classroom and a helpful friend and try to teach the material to them. Outline your notes on the board and talk about them.



Good luck I know you'll do great! Stay focused and get plenty of rest the night before! Don't forget breakfast the day of as well!



</answer>



<answer from>



Paul G
Member since: 21 May 2008
Total points: 296 (Level 2)



</answer from>

</answer>
